</td>
<td> <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrococcus" title="Pyrococcus">Pyrococcus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Methanosarcinales" title="Methanosarcinales">Methanosarcinales</a></i> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some Euryarchaeota are <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a> (archaea that produce methane as a result of metabolism) living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_environment" title="Anaerobic environment">anaerobic environments</a>, such as swamps. This form of metabolism evolved early, and it is even possible that the first free-living organism was a methanogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> A common reaction involves the use of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as an electron acceptor to oxidize <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. Methanogenesis involves a range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzymes</a> that are unique to these archaea, such as <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_M" title="Coenzyme M">coenzyme M</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanofuran" title="Methanofuran">methanofuran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Other organic compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> are used as alternative <a href="/wiki/Electron_acceptor" title="Electron acceptor">electron acceptors</a> by methanogens. These reactions are common in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_(zoology)" title="Gut (zoology)">gut</a>-dwelling archaea. <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">Acetic acid</a> is also broken down into methane and carbon dioxide directly, by <i>acetotrophic</i> archaea. These acetotrophs are archaea in the order <a href="/wiki/Methanosarcinales" title="Methanosarcinales">Methanosarcinales</a>, and are a major part of the communities of microorganisms that produce <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bacteriorhodopsin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="517" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Bacteriorhodopsin.png/170px-Bacteriorhodopsin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Bacteriorhodopsin.png/255px-Bacteriorhodopsin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Bacteriorhodopsin.png/340px-Bacteriorhodopsin.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bacteriorhodopsin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bacteriorhodopsin from <i><a href="/wiki/Halobacterium_salinarum" title="Halobacterium salinarum">Halobacterium salinarum</a></i>. The retinol <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactor</a> and residues involved in proton transfer are shown as <a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">ball-and-stick models</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Other archaea use <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> as a source of carbon, in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a> (they are <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">autotrophs</a>). This process involves either a highly modified form of the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> or another metabolic pathway called the 3-hydroxypropionate/ 4-hydroxybutyrate cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The Thermoproteota also use the <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle" title="Reverse Krebs cycle">reverse Krebs cycle</a> while the "Euryarchaeota" also use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reductive_acetyl-CoA_pathway" title="Reductive acetyl-CoA pathway">reductive acetyl-CoA pathway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Carbon fixation is powered by inorganic energy sources. No known archaea carry out <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bryant_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bryant-140">[140]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Halobacterium" title="Halobacterium">Halobacterium</a> is the only known phototroph archeon but it uses an alternative process to photosynthesis). Archaeal energy sources are extremely diverse, and range from the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Nitrosopumilales" title="Nitrosopumilales">Nitrosopumilales</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-142">[142]</a></sup> to the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> or elemental <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i>, using either oxygen or metal ions as electron acceptors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schafer_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schafer-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">Phototrophic</a> archaea use light to produce chemical energy in the form of ATP. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halobacteria" title="Halobacteria">Halobacteria</a>, light-activated ion pumps like <a href="/wiki/Bacteriorhodopsin" title="Bacteriorhodopsin">bacteriorhodopsin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halorhodopsin" title="Halorhodopsin">halorhodopsin</a> generate ion gradients by pumping ions out of and into the cell across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a>. The energy stored in these <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradients</a> is then converted into ATP by <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergey_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bergey-99">[99]</a></sup> This process is a form of <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">photophosphorylation</a>. The ability of these light-driven pumps to move ions across membranes depends on light-driven changes in the structure of a <a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">retinol</a> <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactor</a> buried in the center of the protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">Plasmid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">Genome</a></div>
<p>Archaea usually have a single <a href="/wiki/Circular_chromosome" title="Circular chromosome">circular chromosome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allers_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allers-144">[144]</a></sup> with as many as 5,751,492 <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Methanosarcina_acetivorans" title="Methanosarcina acetivorans">Methanosarcina acetivorans</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> the largest known archaeal genome. The tiny 490,885 base-pair genome of <i><a href="/wiki/Nanoarchaeum_equitans" title="Nanoarchaeum equitans">Nanoarchaeum equitans</a></i> is one-tenth of this size and the smallest archaeal genome known; it is estimated to contain only 537 protein-encoding genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas.org_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas.org-146">[146]</a></sup> Smaller independent pieces of DNA, called <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmids</a></i>, are also found in archaea. Plasmids may be transferred between cells by physical contact, in a process that may be similar to <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation" title="Bacterial conjugation">bacterial conjugation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SotaTop_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-SotaTop-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RT8-4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="539" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/RT8-4.jpg/170px-RT8-4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/RT8-4.jpg/255px-RT8-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/RT8-4.jpg/340px-RT8-4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RT8-4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i> infected with the <a href="/wiki/DNA_virus" title="DNA virus">DNA virus</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/STSV-1" title="STSV-1">STSV1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Bar is 1 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometer</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Archaea are genetically distinct from bacteria and eukaryotes, with up to 15% of the proteins encoded by any one archaeal genome being unique to the domain, although most of these unique genes have no known function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Of the remainder of the unique proteins that have an identified function, most belong to the Euryarchaeota and are involved in methanogenesis. The proteins that archaea, bacteria and eukaryotes share form a common core of cell function, relating mostly to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a>, <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotide metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaasterland_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaasterland-151">[151]</a></sup> Other characteristic archaeal features are the organization of genes of related function – such as enzymes that catalyze steps in the same <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathway</a> into novel <a href="/wiki/Operon" title="Operon">operons</a>, and large differences in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNA" title="TRNA">tRNA</a> genes and their <a href="/wiki/Aminoacyl_tRNA_synthetase" title="Aminoacyl tRNA synthetase">aminoacyl tRNA synthetases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaasterland_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaasterland-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transcription in archaea more closely resembles eukaryotic than bacterial transcription, with the archaeal <a href="/wiki/RNA_polymerase" title="RNA polymerase">RNA polymerase</a> being very close to its equivalent in eukaryotes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allers_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allers-144">[144]</a></sup> while archaeal translation shows signs of both bacterial and eukaryotic equivalents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennis_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennis-152">[152]</a></sup> Although archaea have only one type of RNA polymerase, its structure and function in transcription seems to be close to that of the eukaryotic <a href="/wiki/RNA_polymerase_II" title="RNA polymerase II">RNA polymerase II</a>, with similar protein assemblies (the <a href="/wiki/General_transcription_factor" title="General transcription factor">general transcription factors</a>) directing the binding of the RNA polymerase to a gene's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)">promoter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> but other archaeal <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> are closer to those found in bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Post-transcriptional_modification" title="Post-transcriptional modification">Post-transcriptional modification</a> is simpler than in eukaryotes, since most archaeal genes lack <a href="/wiki/Intron" title="Intron">introns</a>, although there are many introns in their <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA" title="Ribosomal RNA">ribosomal RNA</a> genes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and introns may occur in a few protein-encoding genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_transfer_and_genetic_exchange">Gene transfer and genetic exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gene transfer and genetic exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Haloferax_volcanii" title="Haloferax volcanii">Haloferax volcanii</a></i>, an extreme halophilic archaeon, forms cytoplasmic bridges between cells that appear to be used for transfer of DNA from one cell to another in either direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the hyperthermophilic archaea <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus_solfataricus" title="Sulfolobus solfataricus">Sulfolobus solfataricus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frols2008_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frols2008-159">[159]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus_acidocaldarius" title="Sulfolobus acidocaldarius">Sulfolobus acidocaldarius</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ajon_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ajon-160">[160]</a></sup> are exposed to DNA-damaging UV irradiation or to the agents <a href="/wiki/Bleomycin" title="Bleomycin">bleomycin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mitomycin_C" title="Mitomycin C">mitomycin C</a>, species-specific cellular aggregation is induced. Aggregation in <i>S. solfataricus</i> could not be induced by other physical stressors, such as pH or temperature shift,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frols2008_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frols2008-159">[159]</a></sup> suggesting that aggregation is induced specifically by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_damage" title="DNA damage">DNA damage</a>. Ajon et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ajon_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ajon-160">[160]</a></sup> showed that UV-induced cellular aggregation mediates chromosomal marker exchange with high frequency in <i>S. acidocaldarius</i>. Recombination rates exceeded those of uninduced cultures by up to three orders of magnitude. Frols et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frols2008_159-2"><a href="#cite_note-Frols2008-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> and Ajon et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ajon_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ajon-160">[160]</a></sup> hypothesized that cellular aggregation enhances species-specific DNA transfer between <i>Sulfolobus</i> cells in order to provide increased repair of damaged DNA by means of <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombination</a>. This response may be a primitive form of sexual interaction similar to the more well-studied bacterial transformation systems that are also associated with species-specific DNA transfer between cells leading to homologous recombinational repair of DNA damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeal_viruses">Archaeal viruses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Archaeal viruses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Archaea are the target of a number of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> in a diverse virosphere distinct from bacterial and eukaryotic viruses. They have been organized into 15-18 DNA-based families so far, but multiple species remain un-isolated and await classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> These families can be informally divided into two groups: archaea-specific and cosmopolitan. Archaeal-specific viruses target only archaean species and currently include 12 families. Numerous unique, previously unidentified viral structures have been observed in this group, including: bottle-shaped, spindle-shaped, coil-shaped, and droplet-shaped viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-164">[164]</a></sup> While the reproductive cycles and genomic mechanisms of archaea-specific species may be similar to other viruses, they bear unique characteristics that were specifically developed due to the morphology of host cells they infect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-163">[163]</a></sup> Their virus release mechanisms differ from that of other phages. <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">Bacteriophages</a> generally undergo either <a href="/wiki/Lytic_cycle" title="Lytic cycle">lytic</a> pathways, <a href="/wiki/Lysogenic_cycle" title="Lysogenic cycle">lysogenic</a> pathways, or (rarely) a mix of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Most archaea-specific viral strains maintain a stable, somewhat lysogenic, relationship with their hosts – appearing as a chronic infection. This involves the gradual, and continuous, production and release of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virions</a> without killing the host cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Prangishyili (2013) noted that it has been hypothesized that tailed archaeal phages originated from bacteriophages capable of infecting <a href="/wiki/Haloarchaea" title="Haloarchaea">haloarchaeal</a> species. If the hypothesis is correct, it can be concluded that other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-stranded_DNA_viruses" title="Double-stranded DNA viruses">double-stranded DNA viruses</a> that make up the rest of the archaea-specific group are their own unique group in the global viral community. Krupovic et al. (2018) states that the high levels of <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a>, rapid mutation rates in viral genomes, and lack of universal gene sequences have led researchers to perceive the evolutionary pathway of archaeal viruses as a network. The lack of similarities among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_marker" title="Phylogenetic marker">phylogenetic markers</a> in this network and the global virosphere, as well as external linkages to non-viral elements, may suggest that some species of archaea specific viruses evolved from non-viral <a href="/wiki/Mobile_genetic_elements" title="Mobile genetic elements">mobile genetic elements</a> (MGE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_164-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>These viruses have been studied in most detail in thermophilics, particularly the orders <a href="/wiki/Sulfolobales" title="Sulfolobales">Sulfolobales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermoproteales" title="Thermoproteales">Thermoproteales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Two groups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-stranded_DNA_viruses" title="Single-stranded DNA viruses">single-stranded DNA viruses</a> that infect archaea have been recently isolated. One group is exemplified by the <i><a href="/wiki/Halorubrum_pleomorphic_virus_1" title="Halorubrum pleomorphic virus 1">Halorubrum pleomorphic virus 1</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Pleolipoviridae" title="Pleolipoviridae">Pleolipoviridae</a></i>) infecting halophilic archaea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> and the other one by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeropyrum_coil-shaped_virus" title="Aeropyrum coil-shaped virus">Aeropyrum coil-shaped virus</a></i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Spiraviridae" title="Spiraviridae">Spiraviridae</a></i>) infecting a hyperthermophilic (optimal growth at 90–95 °C) host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Notably, the latter virus has the largest currently reported ssDNA genome. Defenses against these viruses may involve <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repetitive_DNA" title="Repetitive DNA">repetitive DNA</a> sequences that are related to the genes of the viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">Asexual reproduction</a></div>
<p>Archaea reproduce asexually by binary or multiple <a href="/wiki/Fission_(biology)" title="Fission (biology)">fission</a>, fragmentation, or <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">budding</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> do not occur, so if a species of archaea exists in more than one form, all have the same genetic material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergey_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bergey-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">Cell division</a> is controlled in a <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>; after the cell's <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a> is replicated and the two daughter chromosomes separate, the cell divides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernander_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernander-173">[173]</a></sup> In the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i>, the cycle has characteristics that are similar to both bacterial and eukaryotic systems. The chromosomes replicate from multiple starting points (<a href="/wiki/Origin_of_replication" title="Origin of replication">origins of replication</a>) using <a href="/wiki/DNA_polymerase" title="DNA polymerase">DNA polymerases</a> that resemble the equivalent eukaryotic enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Euryarchaeota the cell division protein <a href="/wiki/FtsZ" title="FtsZ">FtsZ</a>, which forms a contracting ring around the cell, and the components of the <a href="/wiki/Septum" title="Septum">septum</a> that is constructed across the center of the cell, are similar to their bacterial equivalents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernander_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernander-173">[173]</a></sup> In cren-<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> and thaumarchaea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> the cell division machinery Cdv fulfills a similar role. This machinery is related to the eukaryotic ESCRT-III machinery which, while best known for its role in cell sorting, also has been seen to fulfill a role in separation between divided cell, suggesting an ancestral role in cell division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29551994_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29551994-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both bacteria and eukaryotes, but not archaea, make <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Some species of <a href="/wiki/Haloarchaea" title="Haloarchaea">Haloarchaea</a> undergo <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_switching" title="Phenotypic switching">phenotypic switching</a> and grow as several different cell types, including thick-walled structures that are resistant to <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_shock" title="Osmotic shock">osmotic shock</a> and allow the archaea to survive in water at low salt concentrations, but these are not reproductive structures and may instead help them reach new habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">Communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quorum_sensing" title="Quorum sensing">Quorum sensing</a> was originally thought to not exist in Archaea, but recent studies have shown evidence of some species being able to perform cross-talk through quorum sensing. Other studies have shown syntrophic interactions between archaea and bacteria during biofilm growth. Although research is limited in archaeal quorum sensing, some studies have uncovered LuxR proteins in archaeal species, displaying similarities with bacteria LuxR, and ultimately allowing for the detection of small molecules that are used in high density communication. Similarly to bacteria, Archaea LuxR solos have shown to bind to AHLs (lactones) and non-AHLs ligans, which is a large part in performing intraspecies, interspecies, and interkingdom communication through quorum sensing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitats">Habitats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Habitats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg/220px-Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg/330px-Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg/440px-Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaea that grow in the hot water of the Morning Glory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_Spring" title="Hot Spring">Hot Spring</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a> produce a bright colour</div></div></div>
<p>Archaea exist in a broad range of <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a>, and are now recognized as a major part of global <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeLong-18">[18]</a></sup> and may represent about 20% of microbial cells in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> However, the first-discovered archaeans were <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valentine_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-valentine-129">[129]</a></sup> Indeed, some archaea survive high temperatures, often above 100 °C (212 °F), as found in <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_smoker" title="Black smoker">black smokers</a>, and oil wells. Other common habitats include very cold habitats and highly <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">saline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline">alkaline</a> water, but archaea include <a href="/wiki/Mesophile" title="Mesophile">mesophiles</a> that grow in mild conditions, in <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestinal_tract" title="Intestinal tract">intestinal tract</a> of animals, and <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-DeLong-18">[18]</a></sup> Similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_growth-promoting_rhizobacteria" title="Plant growth-promoting rhizobacteria">PGPR</a>, Archaea are now considered as a source of plant growth promotion as well. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Extremophile archaea are members of four main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> groups. These are the <a href="/wiki/Halophile" title="Halophile">halophiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">thermophiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkaliphile" title="Alkaliphile">alkaliphiles</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acidophile_(organisms)" title="Acidophile (organisms)">acidophiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pikuta_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pikuta-183">[183]</a></sup> These groups are not comprehensive or phylum-specific, nor are they mutually exclusive, since some archaea belong to several groups. Nonetheless, they are a useful starting point for classification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28777382_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28777382-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Halophiles, including the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Halobacterium" title="Halobacterium">Halobacterium</a></i>, live in extremely saline environments such as <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a> and outnumber their bacterial counterparts at salinities greater than 20–25%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valentine_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-valentine-129">[129]</a></sup> Thermophiles grow best at temperatures above 45 °C (113 °F), in places such as hot springs; <i>hyperthermophilic</i> archaea grow optimally at temperatures greater than 80 °C (176 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> The archaeal <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methanopyrus_kandleri" title="Methanopyrus kandleri">Methanopyrus kandleri</a></i> Strain 116 can even reproduce at 122 °C (252 °F), the highest recorded temperature of any organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other archaea exist in very acidic or alkaline conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pikuta_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pikuta-183">[183]</a></sup> For example, one of the most extreme archaean acidophiles is <i><a href="/wiki/Picrophilus" title="Picrophilus">Picrophilus torridus</a></i>, which grows at pH 0, which is equivalent to thriving in 1.2 <a href="/wiki/Molar_concentration" title="Molar concentration">molar</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>This resistance to extreme environments has made archaea the focus of speculation about the possible properties of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Some extremophile habitats are not dissimilar to those on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> leading to the suggestion that viable microbes could be transferred between planets in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recently, several studies have shown that archaea exist not only in mesophilic and thermophilic environments but are also present, sometimes in high numbers, at low temperatures as well. For example, archaea are common in cold oceanic environments such as polar seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Even more significant are the large numbers of archaea found throughout the world's oceans in non-extreme habitats among the <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> community (as part of the <a href="/wiki/Picoplankton" title="Picoplankton">picoplankton</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karner_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karner-192">[192]</a></sup> Although these archaea can be present in extremely high numbers (up to 40% of the microbial biomass), almost none of these species have been isolated and studied in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_culture" title="Pure culture">pure culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Consequently, our understanding of the role of archaea in ocean ecology is rudimentary, so their full influence on global <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemistry" title="Biogeochemistry">biogeochemical</a> cycles remains largely unexplored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Some marine Thermoproteota are capable of <a href="/wiki/Nitrification" title="Nitrification">nitrification</a>, suggesting these organisms may affect the oceanic <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-141">[141]</a></sup> although these oceanic Thermoproteota may also use other sources of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vast numbers of archaea are also found in the <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> that cover the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_floor" title="Sea floor">sea floor</a>, with these organisms making up the majority of living cells at depths over 1 meter below the ocean bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> It has been demonstrated that in all oceanic surface sediments (from 1000- to 10,000-m water depth), the impact of viral infection is higher on archaea than on bacteria and virus-induced lysis of archaea accounts for up to one-third of the total microbial biomass killed, resulting in the release of ~0.3 to 0.5 gigatons of carbon per year globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_chemical_cycling">Role in chemical cycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Role in chemical cycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">Biogeochemical cycle</a></div>
<p>Archaea recycle elements such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> through their various habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid29753224_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29753224-199">[199]</a></sup> Archaea carry out many steps in the <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a>. This includes both reactions that remove nitrogen from ecosystems (such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>-based respiration and <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">denitrification</a>) as well as processes that introduce nitrogen (such as nitrate assimilation and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
Researchers recently discovered archaeal involvement in <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> oxidation reactions. These reactions are particularly important in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The archaea also appear crucial for ammonia oxidation in soils. They produce <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">nitrite</a>, which other microbes then oxidize to <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>. Plants and other organisms consume the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeiningerUrich2006_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeiningerUrich2006-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_cycle" title="Sulfur cycle">sulfur cycle</a>, archaea that grow by oxidizing <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds release this element from rocks, making it available to other organisms, but the archaea that do this, such as <i>Sulfolobus</i>, produce <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> as a waste product, and the growth of these organisms in abandoned mines can contribute to <a href="/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage" title="Acid mine drainage">acid mine drainage</a> and other environmental damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker2003_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker2003-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, methanogen archaea remove hydrogen and play an important role in the decay of organic matter by the populations of microorganisms that act as <a href="/wiki/Decomposer" title="Decomposer">decomposers</a> in anaerobic ecosystems, such as sediments, marshes, and <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage-treatment</a> works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_with_other_organisms">Interactions with other organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Interactions with other organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biological_interaction" title="Biological interaction">Biological interaction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="1799" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg/170px-Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg/255px-Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg/340px-Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Methanogenic archaea form a <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> with <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The well-characterized interactions between archaea and other organisms are either <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutual</a> or <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensal</a>. There are no clear examples of known archaeal <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> but some species of methanogens have been suggested to be involved in <a href="/wiki/Periodontal_disease" title="Periodontal disease">infections in the mouth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nanoarchaeum_equitans" title="Nanoarchaeum equitans">Nanoarchaeum equitans</a></i> may be a parasite of another species of archaea, since it only survives and reproduces within the cells of the Crenarchaeon <i><a href="/wiki/Ignicoccus" title="Ignicoccus">Ignicoccus hospitalis</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas.org_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas.org-146">[146]</a></sup> and appears to offer no benefit to its <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutualism">Mutualism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mutualism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mutualism is an interaction between individuals of different species that results in positive (beneficial) effects on per capita reproduction and/or survival of the interacting populations. One well-understood example of <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a> is the interaction between <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic archaea</a> in the digestive tracts of animals that digest <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaban_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaban-211">[211]</a></sup> In these anaerobic environments, protozoa break down plant cellulose to obtain energy. This process releases hydrogen as a waste product, but high levels of hydrogen reduce energy production. When methanogens convert hydrogen to methane, protozoa benefit from more energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>In anaerobic protozoa, such as <i>Plagiopyla frontata</i>, archaea reside inside the protozoa and consume hydrogen produced in their <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenosome" title="Hydrogenosome">hydrogenosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lange2005_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lange2005-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Archaea also associate with larger organisms. For example, the marine archaean <i><a href="/wiki/Cenarchaeum" title="Cenarchaeum">Cenarchaeum symbiosum</a></i> lives within (is an <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiont</a> of) the <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponge</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Axinella" title="Axinella">Axinella mexicana</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Preston1996_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Preston1996-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Commensalism">Commensalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Commensalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Archaea can also be commensals, benefiting from an association without helping or harming the other organism. For example, the methanogen <i><a href="/wiki/Methanobrevibacter_smithii" title="Methanobrevibacter smithii">Methanobrevibacter smithii</a></i> is by far the most common archaean in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_flora" title="Human flora">human flora</a>, making up about one in ten of all the prokaryotes in the human gut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> In termites and in humans, these methanogens may in fact be mutualists, interacting with other microbes in the gut to aid digestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Archaean communities also associate with a range of other organisms, such as on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> and in the region of soil that surrounds plant roots (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhizosphere_(ecology)" title="Rhizosphere (ecology)">rhizosphere</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitism">Parasitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Parasitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although Archaea do not have a historical reputation of being pathogens, Archaea are often found with similar genomes to more common pathogen like <i>E. coli,</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> showing metabolic links and evolutionary history with today's pathogens. Archaea also has inconsistent detection within clinical studies because of the lack of categorization of Archaea into more specific species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance_in_technology_and_industry">Significance in technology and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Archaea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Significance in technology and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">Extremophile</a> archaea, particularly those resistant either to heat or to extremes of acidity and alkalinity, are a source of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that function under these harsh conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egorova_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egorova-224">[224]</a></sup> These enzymes have found many uses. For example, thermostable <a href="/wiki/DNA_polymerase" title="DNA polymerase">DNA polymerases</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pfu_DNA_polymerase" title="Pfu DNA polymerase">Pfu DNA polymerase</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrococcus_furiosus" title="Pyrococcus furiosus">Pyrococcus furiosus</a></i>, revolutionized <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> by allowing the <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> to be used in research as a simple and rapid technique for <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">cloning</a> DNA. In industry, <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galactosidases" title="Galactosidases">galactosidases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pullulanase" title="Pullulanase">pullulanases</a> in other species of <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrococcus" title="Pyrococcus">Pyrococcus</a></i> that function at over 100 °C (212 °F) allow <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a> at high temperatures, such as the production of low <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> milk and <a href="/wiki/Whey" title="Whey">whey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Enzymes from these thermophilic archaea also tend to be very stable in organic solvents, allowing their use in environmentally friendly processes in <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">green chemistry</a> that synthesize organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egorova_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Egorova-224">[224]</a></sup> This stability makes them easier to use in <a href="/wiki/Structural_biology" title="Structural biology">structural biology</a>. Consequently, the counterparts of bacterial or eukaryotic enzymes from extremophile archaea are often used in structural studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast with the range of applications of archaean enzymes, the use of the organisms themselves in biotechnology is less developed. <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">Methanogenic archaea</a> are a vital part of <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, since they are part of the community of microorganisms that carry out <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic digestion</a> and produce <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mineral_processing" title="Mineral processing">mineral processing</a>, acidophilic archaea display promise for the extraction of metals from <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaea host a new class of potentially useful <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>. A few of these <a href="/wiki/Archaeocin" title="Archaeocin">archaeocins</a> have been characterized, but hundreds more are believed to exist, especially within <i><a href="/wiki/Haloarchaea" title="Haloarchaea">Haloarchaea</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfolobus" title="Sulfolobus">Sulfolobus</a></i>. These compounds differ in structure from bacterial antibiotics, so they may have novel modes of action. In addition, they may allow the creation of new <a href="/wiki/Selectable_marker" title="Selectable marker">selectable markers</a> for use in archaeal molecular biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-:2-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:2_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:2_7-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFChowPaddaPuriChanway2022">Chow, Chanelle; Padda, Kiran Preet; Puri, Akshit; Chanway, Chris P.
